    Essential Python components for automation

    Automating Tasks in Python

    Automation is the use of technology to reduce human and manual effort to perform common and repetitive tasks. As a security analyst, you will primarily use Python to automate tasks.

    You have encountered multiple examples of how to use Python for automation in this course, including investigating logins, managing access, and updating devices.

    Automating cybersecurity-related tasks requires understanding the following Python components that you've worked with in this course:

    • Variables: A container that stores data.
    • Conditional statements: Statements that evaluate code to determine if it meets a specified set of conditions.
    • Iterative statements: Code that repeatedly executes a set of instructions.
    • Functions: Sections of code that can be reused in a program.
    • Techniques for working with strings: Including bracket notation, functions, and methods like str(), len(), and .index().
    • Techniques for working with lists: Including bracket notation and methods like .insert(), .remove(), .append(), and .index().

    Example: Counting Logins Made by a Flagged User

    As one example, you may find that you need to investigate the logins of a specific user who has been flagged for unusual activity. Specifically, you are responsible for counting how many times this user has logged in for the day.

    To automate the investigation, you'll need to incorporate a for loop, conditional statement, and a counter variable.

    Working with Files in Python

    Another essential component of automating cybersecurity-related tasks in Python is understanding how to work with files. Security-related data will often be initially found in log files, which can be .txt or .csv files.

    You can easily extract data from .txt and .csv files, as well as convert them into other file formats.

    Coming up, you'll learn how to import, read from, and write to files, as well as how to structure the information contained in files.

    Key Takeaways

    It is important for security analysts to be able to automate tasks in Python. This requires knowledge of fundamental Python concepts, including variables, conditional statements, iterative statements, and techniques for working with strings and lists. In addition, the ability to work with files is also essential for automation in Python.

    Import files into Python

    Working with Files in Cybersecurity

    Security analysts may need to access a variety of files when working in Python. Many of these files will be logs, which record events that occur within an organization's systems.

    For instance, logs might contain information on login attempts or software application issues, which analysts may need to investigate.

    Opening Files in Python

    To open a file in Python for reading, you can use the open() function:

    with open("update_log.txt", "r") as file:

                    
                    # Code to work with the file

    The with keyword manages resources and errors when working with files. The open() function opens the specified file, and the as keyword assigns the opened file to a variable.

    The second parameter of open() specifies the mode of file access. For reading, it's "r".

    Reading Files in Python

    After opening a file, you can read its contents using the .read() method:

    with open("update_log.txt", "r") as file:
                    updates = file.read()
                
                print(updates)

    The .read() method converts the file contents into a string. Once read, you can perform operations on the string as needed.

    Writing Files in Python

    To write to a file, use the open() function with mode "w" for writing or "a" for appending:

    line = "jrafael,192.168.243.140,4:56:27,True"
                
                with open("access_log.txt", "a") as file:
                    file.write(line)

    The .write() method is used to write string data to the file. With "w", the file contents are replaced; with "a", new data is appended to the end.

    It's important for security analysts to be able to import files into Python and then read from or write to them. Understanding file operations is essential for effective automation and analysis tasks.

    Explore debugging techniques

    Types of Errors

    It's a normal part of developing code in Python to encounter errors. Understanding the three main types of errors can help:

    1. Syntax errors: These involve invalid usage of the programming language. They occur when there is a mistake with the Python syntax itself.
    2. Logic errors: These result when the logic used in code produces unintended results. The code will not behave as expected, but it is still valid to the interpreter.
    3. Exceptions: These involve code that cannot be executed even though it is syntactically correct. They occur for various reasons, such as undefined variables or functions.

    Syntax Errors

    A syntax error is identified by the Python interpreter when there is an issue with the syntax of the code. Common examples include missing punctuation marks.

    For example:

    1 message = "You are debugging a syntax error
                2 print(message)

    This code will produce a "SyntaxError: EOL while scanning string literal" because a quotation mark is missing at the end of the string.

    Logic Errors

    Logic errors occur when the code does not produce the expected outcome due to faulty logic. These errors may not generate error messages but result in incorrect behavior.

    For example:

    1 login_attempts = 5
                2 if login_attempts >= 5:
                3    print("User has not reached maximum number of login attempts.")
                4 else:
                    print("User has reached maximum number of login attempts.")

    Although there are five login attempts, the output incorrectly states that the user has not reached the maximum.

    Exceptions

    Exceptions are errors that occur during code execution. They include errors like NameError, IndexError, TypeError, and FileNotFound.

    For example:

    1 username = "elarson"
                2 month = "March"
                3 total_logins = 75
                4 failed_logins = 18
                5 print("Login report for", username, "in", month)
                6 print("Total logins:", total_logins)
                7 print("Failed logins:", failed_logins)
                8 print("Unusual logins:", unusual_logins)

    This code will produce a "NameError" because the variable unusual_logins is not assigned a value.

    Debugging Strategies

    When dealing with errors, the Python interpreter outputs error messages to identify the issues. Here are some strategies for debugging:

    1. Debuggers: Integrated Development Environments (IDEs) offer debugging tools to locate and fix errors. Debuggers use breakpoints and variable tracking to assist in error detection.
    2. Use Print Statements: Adding print statements at various locations in the code helps identify the source of errors. This strategy helps track the flow of execution and variable values.

    By employing these strategies, developers can effectively identify and fix errors in Python code.

    Explore debugging techniques

    Types of Errors in Python

    It's normal to encounter errors while developing code in Python. Understanding the three main types of errors can help you effectively debug your code. These types include syntax errors, logic errors, and exceptions.

    Syntax Errors

    A syntax error occurs due to invalid usage of Python syntax. Common examples include missing punctuation marks or incorrect indentation. When encountering a syntax error, the Python interpreter will provide an error message indicating the location and nature of the error.

    For example:

    message = "You are debugging a syntax error
                print(message)

    This code results in a "SyntaxError: EOL while scanning string literal" due to a missing closing quotation mark.

    Logic Errors

    Logic errors occur when the logic used in code produces unintended results. Unlike syntax errors, logic errors may not produce error messages but lead to incorrect program behavior.

    For example:

    login_attempts = 5
                if login_attempts >= 5:
                    print("User has not reached maximum number of login attempts.")
                else:
                    print("User has reached maximum number of login attempts.")

    This code contains a logic error where it incorrectly outputs that the user has not reached the maximum number of login attempts.

    Exceptions

    An exception is an error that prevents code execution, even if it's syntactically correct. Common exceptions include NameError, IndexError, TypeError, and FileNotFoundError. These errors are accompanied by specific error messages to aid in debugging.

    For example:

    username = "elarson"
                month = "March"
                total_logins = 75
                failed_logins = 18
                print("Login report for", username, "in", month)
                print("Total logins:", total_logins)
                print("Failed logins:", failed_logins)
                print("Unusual logins:", unusual_logins)

    This code results in a NameError due to the use of the undefined variable unusual_logins.

    Debugging Strategies

    Debugging is an essential skill for resolving errors in Python code. Here are some strategies to help you debug effectively:

    Debuggers

    Integrated Development Environments (IDEs) often include debuggers, which help locate errors in code. Debuggers allow you to set breakpoints and inspect variable values during runtime.

    Use Print Statements

    Incorporate temporary print statements strategically to identify the source of errors. Print relevant information at different points in the code to trace the flow and values of variables.

    For example:

    new_users = ["sgilmore", "bmoreno"]
                approved_users = ["bmoreno", "tshah", "elarson"]
                def add_users():
                    for user in new_users:
                        print("line 5 - inside for loop")
                        if user in approved_users:
                            print("line 7 - inside if statement")
                            print(user,"already in list")
                        print("line 9 - before .append method")
                        approved_users.append(user)
                add_users()
                print(approved_users)

    Strategically placed print statements can help identify the flow of execution and isolate logic errors.

    • Conditional statement: A statement that evaluates code to determine if it meets a specified set of conditions
    • Debugger: A software tool that helps to locate the source of an error and assess its causes
    • Debugging: The practice of identifying and fixing errors in code

    • Logic error: An error that results when the logic used in code produces unintended results
    • Parsing: The process of converting data into a more readable format
    • Syntax error: An error that involves invalid usage of a programming language
    • Variable: A container that stores data

    Course 8

    Module 1



    Data and asset classification

    Classifying for Safety

    Security professionals classify data types to properly protect organizations from cyber attacks. Here are common data types:

    • Public data: Already accessible to the public and requires minimal security protections.
    • Private data: Information that should be kept from the public and poses a serious risk if accessed without authorization.
    • Sensitive data: Must be protected from unauthorized access to prevent financial and reputational damage.
    • Confidential data: Important for ongoing business operations and often requires limited access and non-disclosure agreements.

    Asset Classification

    Asset classification labels assets based on sensitivity and importance to an organization:

    • Low-level assets: Public data, readily available to the public without significant negative impact if compromised.
    • High-level assets: Sensitive and confidential data, with potential for significant negative impact on the organization if leaked publicly.

    Understanding different data and asset classification types is crucial for prioritizing data protection efforts and implementing appropriate security measures.

    Disaster recovery and business continuity

    The Importance of Business Continuity and Disaster Recovery Plans

    Security professionals play a crucial role in protecting a company's data and assets. However, security incidents can occur despite preventive measures. Business continuity and disaster recovery plans are essential to minimize the impact of such incidents on an organization's operations.

    Identify and Protect

    Creating business continuity and disaster recovery plans is the final step in a four-part process:

    1. The security team identifies the assets requiring protection.
    2. They assess potential threats to those assets.
    3. Tools and processes are implemented to detect threats.
    4. The business continuity and disaster recovery plans are developed.

    Business Continuity Plan

    A business continuity plan helps sustain business operations during and after disruptions. Here are four essential steps:

    1. Conduct a business impact analysis.
    2. Identify, document, and implement steps for recovering critical business functions.
    3. Organize a business continuity team comprising members from various departments.
    4. Conduct training for the business continuity team to prepare for different risk scenarios.

    Disaster Recovery Plan

    A disaster recovery plan outlines steps to minimize the impact of security incidents and resolve threats. Key steps include:

    1. Implementing recovery strategies for software and hardware.
    2. Identifying applications and data that may be affected by a security incident.

    Key Takeaways

    Business continuity and disaster recovery plans are vital for maintaining an organization's security posture. They ensure that business operations can continue even in the face of security incidents.

    Juliana's story: Asset protection

    Juliana's Story: Asset Protection

    Meet Juliana Soto, who recently completed an online cybersecurity certificate program and was hired as a cybersecurity analyst for Right-On-Time Payment Solutions, a fictional payment processing company allowing individuals to transfer money to friends and family. Right-On-Time also allows companies to accept payments from customers or organizations.

    In this reading, you will begin a three-part journey that follows Juliana as she takes on new roles and responsibilities within the cybersecurity team of her new company.

    Understanding Important Assets

    Juliana decides that one of her first objectives is to gain a better understanding of the most important assets to the company by reviewing various company reading materials that will help her learn what is most valuable to them. On her first day, she is given reading materials to help her familiarize herself with the company. She learns that customers must create unique usernames and passwords and provide their full name or company name to sign up for the service as an individual. Business customers can also sign up for the service if they provide their employee identification number (EIN). Finally, customers must enter their bank account information or debit card number for payments to be accepted.

    Juliana discovers that this company handles a lot of personally identifiable information (PII) from its customers. This kind of information is considered sensitive data. Unauthorized access to it can lead to significant damage to the organization’s finances, its customers, and its reputation. Juliana realizes that the most important asset to this company is customer data.

    Information Lifecycle Strategy

    After finishing the required onboarding materials, she decides to put together an information lifecycle strategy. She learned about this when completing her online cybersecurity certificate program.

    1. The first step in the information lifecycle is to identify the important assets to the company, including sensitive customer information such as PII, financial information, social security numbers, and EINs.
    2. The second step is to assess the security measures in place to protect the identified assets and review the company’s information security policies. There are different components to this step, ranging from vulnerability scanning to reviewing processes and procedures that are already in place. Juliana is new to the company and might not be ready to conduct vulnerability scans.
    3. The third step of the information lifecycle is to protect the identified assets of the organization. Once again, this is only Juliana’s first day on the job. She asks her supervisor if she can observe a more senior security analyst for a day. This will give her the opportunity to learn how the security team monitors the company’s systems and network.
    4. The last step of the security lifecycle is to monitor the security processes that have been implemented to protect the organization’s assets. She contacts her supervisor and gives them a detailed report of what she has learned on her first day. She requests to finish her day by monitoring a few of the systems that are in place. Her supervisor is impressed with her initiative and prepares Juliana to monitor the security systems. What a great first day for Juliana!

    Key Takeaways

    Identifying the important assets of a company is a key security analyst responsibility. Once you identify the assets, it can be helpful to follow the information lifecycle strategy to help ensure those assets are being protected effectively. Reviewing a company’s security policies will also help an analyst understand what is important to the company and how the analyst should be protecting that data.

    Glossary terms from module 1

    Key Security Definitions

    • Business Continuity Plan (BCP): A document that outlines the procedures to sustain business operations during and after a significant disruption
    • Confidential Data: Data that often has limits on the number of people who have access to it
    • Disaster Recovery Plan: A plan that allows an organization’s security team to outline the steps needed to minimize the impact of a security incident
    • Private Data: Information that should be kept from the public
    • Public Data: Data that is already accessible to the public and poses a minimal risk to the organization if viewed or shared by others
    • Security Mindset: The ability to evaluate risk and constantly seek out and identify the potential or actual breach of a system, application, or data
    • Sensitive Data: A type of data that includes personally identifiable information (PII), sensitive personally identifiable information (SPII), and protected health information (PHI)

    Module 2



    Escalate with a purpose

    Security Incident Escalation

    Security incident escalation is the process of identifying a potential security incident and transferring it to a more experienced department or team member. As a security analyst, you’ll be expected to recognize potential issues and report them to the appropriate person.

    Notification of Breaches

    Many countries have breach notification laws that require companies to notify individuals of security breaches involving personally identifiable information (PII). It's important for security analysts to be aware of these laws.

    Low-level Security Issues

    Low-level security issues are risks that do not expose PII but still need to be investigated further. Examples include failed login attempts and unauthorized software downloads.

    The Escalation Process

    Every company has unique escalation protocols detailing who should be notified and how incidents should be escalated. Security analysts should be aware of these protocols and escalate both small and large security issues accordingly.

    Recognize roles and responsibilities during escalation

    Roles in Incident Escalation

    Data Owners

    A data owner decides who can access, edit, use, or destroy specific information and is accountable for the classification, protection, access, and use of company data.

    Data Controllers

    Data controllers determine the procedure and purpose for processing data and ensure compliance with relevant security and privacy regulations.

    Data Processors

    Data processors are responsible for processing data on behalf of data controllers and installing security measures to protect the data.

    Data Custodians

    Data custodians assign and remove access to data, implement security controls, create policies for data storage and transmission, and monitor data security.

    Data Protection Officers (DPOs)

    Data protection officers monitor internal compliance with data protection procedures, advise on data protection obligations, and conduct assessments to ensure data security measures are adequate.

    Key Takeaways

    Incident escalation involves various members of a security team acting together. Entry-level analysts should understand the roles and responsibilities of different team members, which helps in recognizing which incidents should be reported to whom.

    Escalation timing

    Role in Incident Escalation

    Your Decisions Matter

    As an entry-level security analyst, your role is crucial in identifying and escalating potential security incidents to the appropriate team members, helping to prevent major security breaches.

    Trust Your Instincts and Ask Questions

    Confidence in decision-making is important, but it's also crucial to ask questions and seek clarification when necessary. Understanding the organization's escalation policy is key to making informed decisions.

    All Security Events Are Not Equal

    Recognizing the importance of different assets and data within the organization helps prioritize incidents. Incidents impacting essential business operations should take precedence over those that do not.

    Quick Escalation Tips

    • Familiarize yourself with the organization's escalation policy.
    • Always follow the policy guidelines.
    • Don't hesitate to ask questions for clarification.

    Key Takeaways

    Understanding and following the organization's escalation policy is essential for an entry-level security analyst. It helps in identifying, prioritizing, and escalating security incidents effectively.

    Juliana's story: Attention to detail

    Juliana's Security Incident Handling

    Juliana, the cybersecurity analyst at Right-On-Time Payment Solutions, demonstrates attention to detail and adherence to the escalation policy in handling security incidents:

    Accomplishments on the First Day

    • Reviewed company information to understand important assets
    • Identified handling of PII data from customers
    • Developed an information security lifecycle strategy
    • Started monitoring security systems

    Handling Security Incidents

    Juliana responds to alerts of suspicious activities:

    • 10 failed login attempts: Recognizes the significance based on the escalation policy and escalates accordingly.
    • Attempted compromise of bank information system: Identifies the urgency due to the sensitivity of customer data and follows the escalation policy meticulously.

    Supervisor's Impression

    Juliana's supervisor acknowledges her initiative and adherence to guidelines in handling security incidents.

    Key Takeaways

    • Attention to detail is crucial for monitoring and following escalation policies.
    • Understanding the organization's assets helps in prioritizing incident escalations.

    Glossary terms from module 2

    Course 8, Module 2: Terms and Definitions

    Data controller:
    A person that determines the procedure and purpose for processing data.
    Data processor:
    A person that is responsible for processing data on behalf of the data controller.
    Data protection officer (DPO):
    An individual that is responsible for monitoring the compliance of an organization's data protection procedures.
    Escalation policy:
    A set of actions that outlines who should be notified when an incident alert occurs and how that incident should be handled.
    Improper usage:
    An incident type that occurs when an employee of an organization violates the organization’s acceptable use policies.
    Incident escalation:
    The process of identifying a potential security incident, triaging it, and handing it off to a more experienced team member.
    Malware infection:
    An incident type that occurs when malicious software designed to disrupt a system infiltrates an organization’s computers or network.
    Unauthorized access:
    An incident type that occurs when an individual gains digital or physical access to a system or an application without permission.

    Module 3



    The purpose and impact of stakeholders

    Stakeholders in Security: Reading

    Who are stakeholders?

    A stakeholder is defined as an individual or group that has an interest in any decision or activity of an organization. A big part of what you’ll do as a security analyst is report your findings to various security stakeholders.

    Levels of stakeholders

    There are many levels of stakeholders within larger organizations. As an entry-level analyst, you might only communicate directly with a few of them. Although you might not communicate with all of the security stakeholders in an organization, it’s important to have an understanding of who key stakeholders are:

    • A cybersecurity risk manager
    • A Chief Executive Officer (CEO)
    • A Chief Financial Officer (CFO)
    • A Chief Information Security Officer (CISO)
    • An operations manager

    CFOs and CISOs are focused on the big picture, like the potential financial burden of a security incident, whereas other roles like operations managers are more focused on the impact on day-to-day operations. Although you will rarely interact directly with high-level security stakeholders, it’s still important to recognize their relevance.

    Stakeholder communications for entry-level analysts

    Two examples of security stakeholders with whom you might regularly communicate are operations managers and risk managers. When you report to these stakeholders, you'll need to clearly communicate the current security issue and its possible causes.

    From one stakeholder to the next

    Operations managers and risk managers are stakeholders who rely on entry-level analysts and other team members to keep them informed of security events in day-to-day operations. These stakeholders commonly report back to the CISOs and CFOs to give a broader narrative of the organization's overall security picture.

    Key takeaways

    Stakeholders play a major role in ensuring the security of an organization. Entry-level analysts should have a foundational understanding of the different levels of security stakeholders within an organization.

    Communicate effectively with stakeholders

    Effective Communication with Security Stakeholders

    Get to the Point

    Security stakeholders have roles and responsibilities that are time-sensitive and impact the business. Therefore, it's crucial that any communications they receive, and the actions they need to take, are clear. To get to the point in your communications, consider:

    • What do I want this person to know?
    • Why is it important for them to know it?
    • When do they need to take action?
    • How do I explain the situation in a non-technical manner?

    Follow the Protocols

    When joining a security team, learn about the different protocols and procedures for communicating with stakeholders and other members of the organization. Ensure you know what applications and forms of communications are acceptable before initiating communication.

    Communicate with Impact

    Interact with lower-level stakeholders, like operations managers or security risk managers, who are interested in day-to-day operations. Address relevant information that relates to their daily responsibilities, such as anomalies in data logs that you are escalating.

    Communication Methods

    Choose communication channels based on the type of information you’re sharing:

    • Instant messaging
    • Emailing
    • Video calling
    • Phone calls
    • Sharing a spreadsheet of data
    • Sharing a slideshow presentation

    Each situation helps you determine the best means of communication.

    Key Takeaways

    • Communicate only information that is specific to stakeholders' interests and impacts their role in the company.
    • Be mindful of the type of information you’re communicating to determine the best method of communication.

    Create visual dashboards for impactful cybersecurity communications

    Using Visual Dashboards for Effective Communication

    Using Visuals to Communicate Effectively

    Security is about protecting a company from threats that can affect its reputation and finances. Clear communications between stakeholders are crucial for responding to threats quickly and effectively. Visuals help provide decision-makers with actionable information to identify potential risks to the organization's security posture.

    Visual Dashboards

    A visual dashboard displays various types of data quickly in one place. They are useful tools for communicating stories about security events, especially those involving numbers and data. Dashboards can range from simple to complex, depending on the information being communicated.

    Pro tip: Programs like Google Sheets and Apache OpenOffice can be used to create visual dashboards.

    When to Use Visual Communication

    Security is often a team effort, and effective communication is essential. Sometimes, it's best to use visuals to communicate complex information, such as results from internal audits or data analysis. Visual dashboards are particularly effective when communicating information involving numbers and data.

    Key Takeaways

    • Be clear and concise when communicating with stakeholders.
    • Visual dashboards are effective for communicating information involving numbers and data.

    Juliana’s story: Effective communication

    Communicating with Stakeholders after Incidents

    Communicating about Incident #1

    One incident involved an employee being locked out of their account due to multiple failed login attempts. Juliana's manager was asked to provide a report reviewing how many departments have experienced locked employee accounts due to failed login attempts over the last month. Juliana decided to create a visual dashboard to represent the data, focusing on numbers such as the number of employees locked out of their accounts in the last month. This dashboard made it easier for high-level stakeholders to review the incident and determine a course of action.

    Communicating about Incident #2

    The second incident involved an attacker almost compromising a system that stores customers’ private data. The Chief Information Security Officer (CISO) requested more information about this incident, including a detailed report on what processes and procedures worked well during the attack and what could be revised. Juliana put together a detailed document with timelines explaining what happened and included her thoughts on potential improvements. This report was shared with the manager for review.

    Key Takeaways

    • Communications for stakeholders should focus on what matters to them most.
    • Recognize the preferences of each stakeholder to determine the best method of communication.

    Module 4



    Strategies for engaging with the cybersecurity community

    Staying Engaged with the Cybersecurity Community

    Security Organizations and Conferences

    Attending security conferences and joining organizations gives you the opportunity to gain knowledge from seasoned professionals who are constantly seeking out new ways to improve on their security strategies and techniques.

    Find the Right Organization

    What security organization should you join? This question depends on your specific interest in security. Are you someone who wants to focus on reacting to security incidents or preventing them from happening? Are you interested in forensic security or data logging? Do you have aspirations of being a CISO one day? It’s important to have a clear understanding of what your interests are before you narrow down your search for a cybersecurity organization or conference.

    Begin the Search

    Once you understand what your interests are, do a web search for organizations or conferences in your area. For example, you can type in “incident response cybersecurity conferences in my area.” This search will give you a list of cybersecurity conferences focused on incident response. If you’re interested in forensic security, you can type “forensic security organizations in my area” or a similar phrase into your web search engine. No matter what your interests are, you can do a web search online to find a cybersecurity organization or conference focused on that area.

    Use Social Media

    Social media is another great way to find cybersecurity organizations or conferences. LinkedIn®, for example, is a social media platform that connects business professionals with one another. You can use LinkedIn® to find security groups or organizations to join. In the LinkedIn® search bar, you can try search queries such as:

    • “Incident response cybersecurity groups”
    • “Organizations for cybersecurity analysts”

    Be aware of social engineering while using social media to connect with other professionals in the security industry.

    Mailing Lists for Security

    Another great way to stay connected with the security industry is to sign up for different cybersecurity mailing lists. These mailing lists send out information periodically on various security topics. The Cybersecurity & Infrastructure Security Agency (CISA) offers two cybersecurity mailing lists for you to join:

    • A list focused on security threat information, best practices for cybersecurity, and analysis from CISA’s domestic and international security partners
    • A list providing weekly summaries of new vulnerabilities that might pose a risk to an organization’s network

    Key Takeaways

    Attackers are always developing new ways to compromise corporate and personal data from users. Cybersecurity organizations and conferences are a great way for security professionals to stay up-to-date on the latest news, tools, and trends in the industry. Be sure to find organizations that align with your security interests.

    Connect with other cybersecurity professionals

    Tips for Engaging with the Cybersecurity Community

    LinkedIn® with CISOs

    It’s a great idea to follow Chief Information Security Officers (CISOs) on LinkedIn®. CISOs are responsible for being up-to-date on all aspects of security, making their insights valuable. By following CISOs on social media, you can discover useful tips and relevant news that can help progress your cybersecurity career.

    Finding Other Security Professionals on LinkedIn®

    LinkedIn® is an excellent platform to connect with other security professionals, whether they're entry-level analysts or seasoned experts. When reaching out to connect with someone, ensure your message is well-written and provides a clear reason for wanting to connect. Using a conversational tone and avoiding spelling and grammatical errors can make your message more engaging and credible.

    Example LinkedIn® Message:

    “Hi, Tim. I recently completed the Google Cybersecurity Certificate program, and I’d like to connect with other security professionals. It seems like you have a lot of experience in the security industry that I can learn from. Let’s keep in touch!”

    Key Takeaways

    • Attackers are continually evolving, making it crucial to stay ahead of the latest trends in security.
    • Connecting with cybersecurity professionals on social media, particularly CISOs, can provide valuable insights and information.
    • When reaching out to connect with others on LinkedIn®, ensure your message is clear, engaging, and free of errors.

    Glossary terms from module 4

    OWASP Top 10

    The OWASP Top 10 is a globally recognized standard awareness document that lists the top 10 most critical security risks to web applications.

    Module 5



    Tips for finding and applying for a job

    Job Search Resources

    ZipRecruiter

    ZipRecruiter is a popular website for job seekers and employers worldwide. It helps connect job seekers with available roles in their industry. You can fill out specific geographical and work preference questions to get matched with opportunities and upload your resume on the platform.

    Indeed

    Indeed is another popular website that connects job seekers with available roles. You can search for jobs using keywords, company names, or job titles, and specify your preferred job location. Uploading your resume on Indeed allows recruiters to reach out to you if your profile matches a job opportunity.

    Monster®

    Monster is a frequently used website internationally that helps connect job seekers with available roles. Similar to other job search sites, you can search for roles and specify your preferred location. Uploading your resume to Monster enables recruiters to contact you if your profile aligns with a job opportunity.

    LinkedIn®

    LinkedIn is a professional networking site where you can find jobs in the cybersecurity field. Visit the "jobs" tab and enter your preferred job location and title. LinkedIn also provides insights into company culture, values, and community initiatives, helping you assess if a company is the right fit for you.

    The interview process

    Preparing for the Interview Process

    Getting Contacted by a Recruiter

    After applying for a job, you might receive a call, email, or message from a recruiter expressing interest in your application. Reply promptly to show your interest.

    Preliminary Interview or Phone Screening

    The first step is often a preliminary interview, which could be conducted over the phone, in person, or via video call. Prepare questions to ask about the role and the company, and send a thank-you email to the recruiter afterward.

    Additional Interviews

    If you pass the preliminary interview, you may be invited for multiple additional interviews, including meetings with the hiring manager and other team members. Always send thank-you notes after each round of interviews.

    Final Offer

    Upon receiving a job offer, decide whether to accept it or negotiate for better terms. Ask for time to make your decision if needed.

    Building Perseverance

    If you don't get the job, express appreciation for the opportunity and ask for feedback. Stay resilient in your job search.

    Key Takeaways

    Interviews are opportunities to learn more about the job and company. Prepare thoroughly and persevere in your job search to build your career in cybersecurity.

    Prepare for technical interviews

    Preparing for Technical Interviews in Cybersecurity

    What are Technical Interviews?

    Technical interviews in cybersecurity focus on assessing your knowledge of specific tools and concepts relevant to the field.

    Python

    Basic knowledge of Python is often expected in cybersecurity roles. Python is valued for its ease of use and extensive libraries, particularly for automation tasks.

    General Techniques

    Demonstrating familiarity with security frameworks like the NIST Cybersecurity Framework and understanding network security concepts are important during technical interviews.

    Writing down the question before answering can help ensure you address all parts of the question and provide a structured response.

    Possible Technical Interview Questions

    • What is the TCP/IP model?
      Answer: The TCP/IP model is a framework for organizing and transmitting data across a network.
    • What is the OSI model?
      Answer: The OSI model describes the seven layers used for computer communication and data transmission.
    • What are SIEM tools and their purpose?
      Answer: SIEM tools, or Security Information and Event Management tools, are used to identify and analyze security threats, risks, and vulnerabilities.

    Key Takeaways

    Technical interviews provide an opportunity to showcase your cybersecurity skills, including coding abilities. Preparation and enthusiasm for learning and applying cybersecurity concepts are essential for success in technical interviews.

    Apply the STAR method during interviews

    Using the STAR Method in Interviews

    The STAR Method

    When interviewing for a job, it can be challenging to convey the right details about your professional history and skills to your interviewers. Using the STAR method can help you share your success stories effectively and strategically. STAR stands for Situation, Task, Action, and Result. Using this method enables you to describe potential challenges you faced in previous roles and gives you the opportunity to show how you thoughtfully approached solving those problems from start to finish.

    Situation

    The situation is the project you worked on or a challenge that you had to overcome. Fully describing the situation allows the interviewer to gain a clear understanding of the challenge you had to overcome.

    Task

    The task outlines the key responsibilities or role you played in solving the challenge described in the situation phase of the STAR method. Specifying what the task is provides clarity about what your objectives were in this scenario.

    Action

    The action describes the exact steps you took to resolve the challenging situation you described in the beginning of the STAR method. Employers want employees who can think fast and make decisions that help solve problems.

    Result

    Finally, sharing the result of your challenge or example shows the employers how the situation was resolved as a direct result of the actions you took. Positive results show an employer that you are someone who has demonstrated an ability to successfully resolve issues.

    Key Takeaways

    • The STAR method stands for Situation, Task, Action, and Result.
    • Using this method helps you communicate to an employer an example of a challenge you faced in the workplace.
    • Remember to use one of your success stories when using the STAR method on an interview.
    • Being able to demonstrate an ability to overcome any type of challenge is a great trait to show off during an interview.
    • Since cybersecurity is such a team-driven industry, effective communication during interviews is crucial.

    Prepare for interviews

    Preparing for Job Interviews in Cybersecurity

    Prepare for the Introductory Call

    It’s important to showcase your best self in the introductory phone call. In this conversation, you’ll talk with the recruiter or hiring manager about yourself, your work or training experience, and why you want the job. Be prepared to discuss your salary requirements.

    Do Your Research

    Make sure you’ve done your research on the company. When asked why you’d be a good fit for the job, employers want to know why you’re interested in cybersecurity and why you want to work specifically at their company.

    Prepare for the Second Round

    Your second-round interview will focus more on what you can offer as an entry-level security analyst. Review your accomplishments in the security industry, and if you have no prior experience, discuss what you’ve learned in this certificate program.

    Panel Interview

    During the panel interview, engage with each panelist and give them your full attention. Address the whole panel when answering questions. Remember that the team brought you in for the interview for a reason, so showcase your knowledge and ability to work well with a team.

    More Resources to Help You Prepare

    • Interview tips from Google: This resource provides best practices and advice on how to prepare and ace your interviews at Google.
    • Interviewing techniques for persons with disabilities: This resource offers helpful advice on navigating the interview process for individuals with disabilities.

    Key Takeaways

    Preparation for your first interview is very important, so be sure to do your research and practice for the introductory call. Don’t worry if you don’t have prior security experience. Instead, rely on the information and skill sets you’ve gained from completing this certificate program.

    Prepare for interviews with Interview Warmup

    Interview Warmup for Cybersecurity

    Interview Warmup is a tool designed to help you practice answering interview questions to become more confident and comfortable throughout the interview process.

    A Practice Question on Interview Warmup

    Get started by following these steps to start a five-question practice interview related to cybersecurity:

    1. Go to grow.google/certificates/interview-warmup/.
    2. Click Start practicing.
    3. Select Cybersecurity to open an additional menu.
    4. Click Start.

    The interview lasts about 10 minutes and includes two background questions, one behavioral question, and two technical questions. You are encouraged to try as many practice interviews as you want.

    You can also review complete lists of cybersecurity interview questions or general interview questions if you'd like to focus on a particular topic.

    How It Works

    Interview Warmup asks you interview questions to practice answering verbally. Your answers will be transcribed in real time, allowing you to review how you responded. In addition, Interview Warmup's machine learning algorithm can detect insights that can help you learn more about your answers and improve the way you communicate.

    Here are a few examples of questions Interview Warmup might ask:

    • What are your career goals for the next five years?
    • What processes can you use to ensure user data is protected?
    • Name two types of common cybersecurity attacks?
    • What are two internal factors that can increase the chances of security risks?
    • What do security information and event management tools enable security analysts to do?
    • In Python, what’s the difference between break and continue?

    Here are some of the insights that Interview Warmup provides:

    • Talking points: The tool lets you know which topics you covered in your answer, such as your experience, skills, and goals. You’ll also be able to view other topics that you might want to consider covering.
    • Most-used words: The tool highlights the words you used most often and suggests synonyms to broaden your word choices.
    • Job-related terms: The tool highlights the words you used that are related to the role or industry in which you are preparing to work. You’ll also be able to view an entire list of job-related terms that you might want to consider including in your answer.

    Interview Warmup gives you the space to practice and prepare for interviews on your own. Your responses will be visible only to you, and they won’t be graded or judged.

    Key Takeaways

    Practicing for interviews is an important skill for your career in cybersecurity. Using Interview Warmup can help you practice interview questions and receive feedback in real time. As you practice, you will gain confidence and be able to prepare more polished responses for common interview questions.

    Learn more about developing an elevator pitch

    Elevator Pitch for Cybersecurity Professionals

    An elevator pitch is a brief summary of your experience, skills, and background that should be communicated in 60 seconds or fewer. Use it to sell yourself as a professional to potential employers.

    Provide an Introduction

    Start by introducing yourself and giving a brief overview of your professional background. Mention some past roles and skills used for those roles that can translate to success in the security field.

    Describe Your Career Interests and Transferable Skills

    Clarify that cybersecurity is your desired career and highlight transferable skills such as problem-solving, communication, and time management.

    Express Your Excitement

    Share your passion for the field and why you want to work in the industry. Talk about your goals and how you want to contribute to defending against threats.

    Communicate Your Interest in the Company

    Explain why you are interested in the company, not just the role. Show that you've done your research and establish a rapport with the interviewer.

    Key Takeaways

    Creating an elevator pitch is a great tool to quickly share who you are. Use it to introduce yourself to career and business connections, and even in other situations like meeting new friends or colleagues.

    Tips for interviewing remotely

    Tips for Remote Interviews

    A remote interview is an interview conducted virtually using video platform software. This type of interview provides an opportunity to connect with hiring managers and recruiters, even if you are not able to meet with them in person. Remote interviews also present challenges that in-person interviews do not, such as issues related to technology, lighting, and sound.

    Test your technology

    The first tip is to test the technology you’ll be using for the video. Different companies use different video platform software to host their remote interviews. Typically, the recruiter or hiring manager will reach out to you over the phone or email to share information about which software will be used for the interview.

    Once you find out which software the company you’re interviewing with uses, you should download that software, if you don't have it already. Next, it's important to test your computer’s camera and microphone to ensure they work well with the video platform software a day or two before the interview. This allows you to resolve any technical issues you might have. Be mindful of how to mute and unmute your microphone, just in case there is noise in your environment that you do not want the interviewer to hear. It’s also important to talk with the recruiter or hiring manager about a backup plan if the technology does not cooperate when it's time for the interview.

    You’ll also want to test any technologies you need to use to ensure you are ready to interview, such as the closed caption feature on the app. Employers are typically happy to accommodate your needs if you're using assistive technology or need specific accommodations. If your internet service is not fast enough to allow for a video interview, you can request a phone interview instead.

    Practice communicating through video

    Communicating through video can be a challenge because there is a slight sound delay. The sound delay can make it difficult to know how long to wait for someone to stop speaking and for you to start. If you don’t have experience communicating through video, consider practicing with friends and family before the remote interview. This will help you learn how pauses affect video communication.

    Create a professional background

    Review your video background before the interview. Typically, you should avoid having an unorganized background or any objects that might distract the interviewer. When interviewing remotely, ensure that your area is well lit. You might want to rearrange your desk or furniture to ensure good lighting.

    Always try to have light behind your camera so that it will shine on your face. If you can’t position your desk next to a window or don’t have enough light coming from the window, consider using artificial light.

    Additionally, you should do your best to limit background noise and use a headset, if possible.

    Dress appropriately

    It’s a good idea to research the company you’re interviewing with to determine which type of interview outfit is suitable. You might need to wear formal business attire during your remote interview for a particular role and company, whereas for another position, more casual clothing might be appropriate. Typically, it is better to overdress than to underdress, especially for more traditional businesses.

    Look at the interviewer when speaking

    When communicating through video, try to look at the interviewer when speaking instead of at the camera. Looking at the interviewer can give them the feeling that you’re engaged in the conversation and focused on what they’re saying.

    Sign in early

    Before the interview, test your technology. This will help you feel confident that everything will work. However, technology and software can be unpredictable. If possible, sign in to your remote interview early to ensure everything is working properly.

    Signing in early also indicates to your interviewer that you respect their time and are a punctual person.

    Key takeaways

    Follow the tips in this reading to become more confident with the remote interviewing process. Always test your technology before the interview to ensure it works well with the video platform software being used for the interview.

    Glossary terms from module 5

    Terms from Course 8, Module 5

    Rapport

    A friendly relationship in which the people involved understand each other’s ideas and communicate well with each other.

    STAR Method

    An interview technique used to answer behavioral and situational questions.

    Elevator Pitch

    A brief summary of your experience, skills, and background.
